LEISURE & ARTS
Urban Development: Towering Controversy
By Willard Spiegelman
1241 words
20 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
D5
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Dallas -- This city loves business; this city loves art. The two enterprises often work in sync. And, as Dallas comes out of a recession, the cranes have returned to the landscape.

But on one front the news is not happy. Former friends no longer speak to one another. Collision has replaced cooperation and threatens civic harmony. The Nasher Sculpture Center, which opened in 2003, is a jewel in the crown of Dallas's downtown arts district. It is now embroiled in a fight with the owners and builders of Museum Tower, a $200 million, 42-story, luxury high-rise condominium, scheduled for completion and occupancy in December. Dallas has always suffered from a lack of downtown residential offerings. So why should this handsome building be considered a threat? Can beauty be evil? Is David confronting Goliath?

It's a complicated Dickensian story involving land rights, real-estate transactions, gentlemen's agreements, accusations of bad faith, egos and, most of all, light, heat and shadows. The end is nowhere in sight, and each party is casting aspersions and blame on the other.

Raymond and Patsy Nasher amassed, over a 50-year period, a great collection of 20th-century sculpture. Wooed by suitors from all over, Mr. Nasher finally decided in 1997 to leave his collection in the city where he made his real-estate fortune. He commissioned a subdued, tranquil museum by Renzo Piano, which houses and rotates pieces from the collection both inside its perfectly lighted bays and outside in an elegant garden.

Across the street, an unimproved parcel of land, purchased by local developers, might have become an extension of the Nasher, but in 2005 the Dallas Police and Fire Pension System stepped in as an investor in a possible residential project. Several architectural plans were submitted and rejected; a nonbinding covenant (which ended in 2008) declared that any building would not exceed 22 stories, and would use glass of no more than 15% reflectivity in order to protect the grounds and interior spaces of the museum. Mr. Piano's ingenious roof for the Nasher involves a series of north-facing oculi, little egg-shell apertures that admit diffused light into the building and cast a soft glow throughout.

In 2007 the pension fund trustees approved a new design for the much larger tower. That same year, Mr. Nasher died at age 85 (Mrs. Nasher had died in 1988). Then came the financial meltdown of 2008, halting construction and sales. The economy edged slowly back, and in 2010 ground-breaking took place for the new tower. A year later, Sculpture Center officials saw reflections bouncing off the tower's glaring glass exterior into the museum. They also closed down a James Turrell sky-viewing room at the end of the garden because the tower had begun to overwhelm its opening to the sky.

By the beginning of this year, they also claim to have noticed that the grass and trees in their garden were being burned by the tower's massive windows. (A 2010 agreement called for material of no more than 35% reflectivity.) According to Jeremy Strick, the Nasher's director, the reflectivity of the glass in Museum Tower is 44%. Things are heating up, indoors and out.

This year both parties met to begin working toward some sort of solution. Negotiations soon turned sour; squabbles ensued. The tower people wanted the museum to modify its roof. The museum replied by saying, essentially, "we were here first." Tom Luce, a local lawyer and civic leader, agreed to act as a mediator. Late last month he stepped aside in frustration.

Only 15 of the 126 apartments (priced at $1.3 million to $4.5 million, not including the $20 million penthouse) have sold. Some potential local buyers may have been scared away because they don't want to suffer criticism that they are helping to damage the very arts district that appealed to them in the first place. A spokesman for the tower claims that once it is occupied the building will generate $9 million annually in tax revenues for the city of Dallas. But no one knows when that may happen.

Any solution will be costly and difficult. The Nasher people have recommended louvers covering the tower's south face. The tower people say that this will require a computer-generated engine for every window, about two years to study, even more time to install. And it may not work. They say they worry about "structural load" and the effect of winds on the 1,000 panes of glass facing the museum. The Nasher says that the louvers are both unobtrusive and eminently workable; they have been used in Germany to good effect. Wind presents no danger.

The tower people have brought forth two experimental solutions. The first is a form of nanotechnology that will coat and harden all the windows with a carbon-based spray. The Nasher officials call it "pixie dust."

The second is a bizarre plan for an almost 400-foot construction-- part sculpture, part machine-- by the architect Joshua Prince-Ramus, to stand between the two buildings and to diffuse the light. It looks like a multidimensional arch that would open and close as needed in order to control glare. The tower people consider this a bold statement that will add to the panache of the arts district. The Nasher people say no: It will be wildly expensive ($20 million to $30 million), and it will overwhelm, as well as overshadow, their delicate museum, what Mr. Piano refers to as "my little gallery." Goliath threatens David once more.

The tower people also say the least expensive solution will be for the museum to redirect the Piano roof apparatus so that light from the high-rise does not enter the galleries. Museum officials say that this plan, in addition to insulting Mr. Piano, will lower the inside illumination by 50% and do nothing to mitigate the effects of light and heat on the gardens. To which the tower people reply that damage to the garden at the Nasher has been minimal, that the Kentucky bluegrass carpeting the lawn is ecologically inappropriate for the Texas climate, and that the trees look just fine. Each side has hired its own consultants to evaluate the damage to the landscape; not surprisingly, they have come up with differing conclusions.

Mr. Strick says that "the creation of the Nasher Sculpture Center, making it a home for the Nasher collection, is the single largest philanthropic gift in the history of Dallas, a legacy that exceeds a billion dollars." He insists gently: "We are determined to preserve it."

When the tower is finished, the owners of the immense penthouse will have a 360-degree view of Dallas, the prairie beyond, and also of a still largely un-residential downtown that includes work by seven Pritzker Award-winning architects: Santiago Calatrava, Norman Foster, Philip Johnson, Rem Koolhass, Thom Mayne, I.M. Pei and Mr. Piano. What they won't see is the very building that has caused such local consternation: their own. Whether Museum Tower will ultimately be considered a work of beauty, a symbol of aggressive capitalism, a white elephant or some combination, remains to be known.

---

Mr. Spiegelman writes about the arts for the Journal.

U.S. Housing Starts Hit Highest Rate Since July 08
By Sarah Portlock and Jeffrey Sparshott
567 words
20 November 2012
09:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.S. home building rose in October to its highest rate in more than four years, the latest sign that the once-troubled housing market is improving.

Housing starts increased 3.6% last month from September to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 894,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. Compared with a year earlier, new home construction was up 41.9%. It is at its highest rate since July 2008, before the depths of the financial crisis.

The Commerce Department said superstorm Sandy had a "minimal" effect on the data because the storm hit only a small part of the country and at the end of the month.

Construction of single-family homes, which made up two-thirds of housing starts last month, dropped 0.2% in October to a rate of 594,000 units. Single-family construction was up 35.3% from a year earlier.

The figures were above expectations. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had forecast overall housing starts would drop to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 833,000, which would have been a 4.5% decline from September's previously reported figures.

Starts in September were revised down to a rate of 863,000, reflecting a 15.1% increase from August.

The numbers are the latest indication the housing market is getting back on track.

Sales of previously owned homes grew 2.1% in October, marking the 16th consecutive month of year-over-year increases, the National Association of Realtors said Monday.

Spending on home construction and home improvements has contributed to economic growth in six straight quarters, the Commerce Department said last month.

A separate report Monday showed U.S. home builders are more optimistic. A reading of their confidence rose for the seventh consecutive month in November to reach its highest level in more than six years, the National Association of Home Builders said. The report also said they are seeing a jump in prospective buyers at a level not seen since April 2006.

While this year's housing starts are up from a low of 478,000 in April 2009, they are still below the historical average. Builders have started construction on about 1.5 million new homes per year since 1959.

The Commerce Department said homes that are razed because of Sandy but will be rebuilt on the existing foundation will count as new housing starts in future data.

Tuesday's report showed that the number of new building permits, an indication of future construction, fell by 2.7% to an annualized level of 866,000 in October. That was above economists' estimates for a rate of 860,000.

Meanwhile, the Commerce data showed new building was up in two of four U.S. regions. Building rose 17.2% in the West and 8.9% in the Midwest, but dropped 6.5% in the Northeast and 2.5% in the South.

Actual housing starts, which are calculated without seasonal adjustments, inched up slightly to 77,900 from a downwardly revised 77,800 in September. Lumber and commodities markets watch those numbers closely to gauge demand.

The Commerce report can be found at http://www.census.gov/construction/nrc[http://www.census.gov/construction/nrc].

Commercial Property: What's the Deal
807 words
19 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

'Affordable' Housing

On the West Side

Just weeks away from breaking ground on the first building in its mega-Hudson Yards development, Related Cos. has lined up financing for a residential project across the street.

New York state's Public Authority Control Board authorized up to $54 million in tax-exempt bonds for the planned 139-unit tower at 529 W. 29th St. The project is eligible for tax-exempt bonds because Related plans to rent some of the units to low and moderate income tenants at so-called "affordable" rents that don't exceed a percentage of their incomes.

The entire project will cost $85.8 million with the rest of the money coming from low-income tax credits and a $33.7 million equity contribution from Related. A spokeswoman for Related declined to discuss the financing but she said Related plans to open the building sometime in 2014.

In August, Related received approval from Chelsea's Community Board 4 on the company's proposal to transfer more than 100 units of affordable housing from the Manhattan Plaza project at 400 and 484 W. 43rd St., to the new development at 529 W. 29th St., the spokeswoman said. She said the developer is still working to get the necessary government approvals to allow the West 29th Street project to be 100% affordable.

The West 29th Street site is less than a block away from another residential tower Related is building with developer Abingdon Properties at 500 W. 30th St. That structure will be a 32-story building with 386 planned units, 77 of which will be affordable.

Both structures are located at the edge of Related's Hudson Yards project, a 26-acre mixed-use mega development planned for Manhattan's West Side. Related plans to break ground in the next few weeks on the first building in the development, a 46-story office tower that will be anchored by Coach Inc.

-- Laura Nahmias

Stormy Relations

Relations between many Manhattan landlords and their tenants got testy in the aftermath of Sandy, which displaced occupants of scores of buildings while saddling owners with the monumental chore of getting them back in commission.

But relations between the tenants of the apartment building at 90 Washington St. and its owner, developer Joseph Moinian, were particularly strained. Tenants of the 398-unit rental tower bombarded his company, Moinian Group, and online websites with complaints and also organized an 80-person email list to keep one another up-to-date.

One of their gripes: a press release that Mr. Moinian put out stating that his company was providing rent credits to its tenants displaced by Sandy and that it was "dedicating all our resources to the speedy return of our tenants."

To many tenants of the rental tower, this added insult to injury partly because Moinian was required by their leases to grant them credits. They also they felt Mr. Moinian's company wasn't doing enough for them and wasn't sensitive to their plight.

Some renters complained they couldn't get any information about the building's status for days. Natan Edelsburg, 25, and his girlfriend Ali Feinstein, 23, say they were concerned about the security of their belongings but onsite staff initially had no information about whether guards would be posted.

Carmine Montalto, 47, says November rent was automatically withdrawn from his account, despite the fact that he didn't have access to his apartment.

"How cold can you possibly be," he asked.

Eric Gerard, spokesman for the Moinian, did the best that it could following "an unprecedented event." He noted that the company established a special hotline for tenants on Nov. 6, as well as a storm-update email address but that "many tenant's questions just could not be answered until the extent of the damage was evaluated." He also said the company hired additional security personnel at the onset of the storm.

Mr. Gerard said that some of the November rent bills were sent out prior to the storm. But he noted that the company was now giving rent credits for December.

Mr. Gerard also pointed out that Mr. Moinian also owns six office buildings and the W New York-Downtown hotel, but the multitude of complaints after the storm came from the residents of 90 Washington.

He declined to comment on the company's decision to put out the press release.

The good news is that tenants of some of the 90 Washington's floors were able to start returning home Saturday. An email sent to them said that the Moinian Group "would like to offer our sincerest appreciation for the patience our residents have shown during this ordeal."

-- Roland Li
